<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Family_law" title="Template:Family law"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Family_law" title="Template talk:Family law"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Family_law&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Cohabitation</b> is an arrangement where two people who are not married live together.</p>
<p>Such arrangements have become increasingly common in Western countries during the past few decades, being led by changing social views, especially regarding marriage, gender roles and religion. They often involve a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_relationship" title="Romantic relationship">romantic relationship</a> and/or sexually <a href="/wiki/Intimate_relationship" title="Intimate relationship">intimate relationship</a> on a long-term or permanent basis.</p>
<p>More broadly, the term <i>cohabitation</i> can mean any number of people living together. To "cohabit", in a broad sense, means to "coexist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-1">[1]</a></sup> The origin of the term comes from the mid 16th century, from the Latin <i>cohabitare</i>, from co- 'together' + habitare 'dwell'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-1">[1]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_changes_leading_to_increase">Social changes leading to increase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Social changes leading to increase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Non_marital_by_countries.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="440" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Non_marital_by_countries.gif" width="440"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Non_marital_by_countries.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentage of births to unmarried women, selected countries, 1980 and 2007<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-non_mar1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-non_mar1-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Today, cohabitation is a common pattern among people in the Western world.</p>
<p>In Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> countries have been the first to start this leading trend, although many countries have since followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Mediterranean Europe has traditionally been very conservative, with religion playing a strong role. Until the mid-1990s, cohabitation levels remained low in this region, but have since increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the past decades, in Western countries, there has been an increase in unmarried couples cohabiting. Historically, many Western countries have been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_sexuality#Christianity" title="Religion and sexuality">Christian doctrines on sex</a>, which opposed unmarried cohabitation. As social mores have changed, such beliefs have become less widely held by the population and some Christian denominations today view cohabitation as a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage" title="Christian views on marriage">marriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2005_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2005-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> has married a cohabiting couple who had children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> while former <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Williams" title="Rowan Williams">Rowan Williams</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and the Archbishop of York <a href="/wiki/John_Sentamu" title="John Sentamu">John Sentamu</a> have expressed tolerance of cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent decades high rates of participation of women in the workforce, and the widespread availability of highly effective <a href="/wiki/Long-acting_reversible_contraception" title="Long-acting reversible contraception">long acting reversible contraceptives</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> has led to women making individual choices over their reproduction with decreased reliance on male partners for financial stability. All these changes favored living arrangement alternatives to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Central and Eastern Europe, during the late 1980s and early 1990s, there were major political changes, such as the fall of Communist governments. These societies entered a new era of increased social freedom, less rigid rules, and less authoritarian governments. They interacted with Western Europe and some became members of the European Union. As a result, the patterns of family life have started to change: marriage rates have declined, and marriage was postponed to a later age. Cohabitation and births to unmarried mothers increased, and in some countries the increase was very quick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>deinstitutionalization of marriage</i> refers to the weakening of the social and legal norms that regulate peoples' behavior in regard to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The rise in cohabitation is part of other major social changes such as: higher divorce rate, older age at first marriage and childbearing, and more births outside marriage. Factors such as secularization, increased participation of women in the labor force, changing in the meaning of marriage, risk reduction, individualism, and changing views on sexuality have been cited as contributing to these social changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> There has also been a change in modern <a href="/wiki/Sexual_ethics" title="Sexual ethics">sexual ethics</a>, with a focus on consent, rather than marital status (i.e. decriminalization of <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">fornication</a>; criminalization of <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>), reflecting new concepts about the role and purpose of sexual interaction, and new conceptualizations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_sexuality" title="Female sexuality">female sexuality</a> and of self-determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> There have been objections against the legal and social regulation of female sexuality; with such regulations being often seen as violations of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In addition, some individuals may feel that marriage is unnecessary or outdated, leading to couples not formalizing their relation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-researchgate.net_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-researchgate.net-16">[16]</a></sup> For instance, in the European Values Study (EVS) of 2008, the percentage of respondents who agreed with the assertion that "Marriage is an outdated institution" was 37.5% in Luxembourg, 35.4% in France, 34.3% in Belgium, 31.2% in Spain, 30.5% in Austria, 29.2% in Germany, 27.7% in Switzerland, 27.2% in Bulgaria, 27.0% in the Netherlands, 25.0% in Slovenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fact that many couples choose to live together without formalizing their relation is also recognized by the European Union. A 2004 directive forbids EU members from denying entry or residence of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">partners</a> "with whom the Union citizen has a durable relationship, duly attested."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>A reason for the more permissive and less traditional views on cohabiting would be a low sex ratio. A low sex ratio would mean there are many more women than men, resulting in differing societal values and acceptable behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reasons_for_cohabitation_in_the_United_States">Reasons for cohabitation in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Reasons for cohabitation in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation_in_the_United_States" title="Cohabitation in the United States">Cohabitation in the United States</a></div>
<p>Today, cohabitation in the United States is often seen as a natural step in the dating process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kramer_2004_28_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer_2004_28-20">[20]</a></sup> In fact, "cohabitation is increasingly becoming the first coresidential union formed among young adults."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2002_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2002-21">[21]</a></sup> More than two-thirds of married couples in the US say that they lived together before getting married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-22">[22]</a></sup> "In 1994, there were 3.7 million cohabiting couples in the United States."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This is a major increase from a few decades ago. According to Dr. Galena Rhoades, "Before 1970, living together outside of marriage was uncommon, but by the late 1990s at least 50% to 60% of couples lived together premaritally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>People may live together for a number of reasons. Cohabitants could live together in order to save money, because of the convenience of living with another, or a need to find housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kramer_2004_28_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer_2004_28-20">[20]</a></sup> Lower income individuals facing financial uncertainty may delay or avoid marriage, not only because of the difficulty of paying for a wedding<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smock2004_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smock2004-25">[25]</a></sup> but also because of fear of financial hardship if a marriage were to end in divorce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22822285_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22822285-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>When given a survey of the reasons why they cohabit, most couples listed reasons such as spending more time together, convenience based reasons, and testing their relationships, while few gave the reason that they do not believe in marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The extremely high costs of housing and tight budgets of today's economy are also factors that can lead a couple to cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today sixty percent of all marriages are preceded by a period of cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohabitation_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohabitation-28">[28]</a></sup> Researchers suggest that couples live together as a way of trying out marriage to test compatibility with their partners, while still having the option of ending the relationship without legal implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> "More than three-quarters of all cohabitors report plans to marry their partners, which implies that most of them view cohabitation as a prelude to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">[30]</a></sup> Cohabitation shares many qualities with marriage, often couples who are cohabiting share a residence, personal resources, exclude intimate relations with others and, in more than 10% of cohabiting couples, have children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-30">[30]</a></sup> "Many young adults believe cohabitation is a good way to test their relationships prior to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Couples who have plans to marry before moving in together or who are engaged before cohabiting typically marry within two years of living together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murrow_2010_397.E2.80.93412_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murrow_2010_397.E2.80.93412-32">[32]</a></sup> The state of cohabitation of a couple often ends either in marriage or in break-up; according to a 1996 study about 10% of cohabiting unions remained in this state more than five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> According to a survey done by The National Center for Health Statistics, "over half of marriages from 1990-1994 among women began as cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2002_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2002-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cohabitation can be an alternative to marriage in situations where marriage is not possible for financial or family reasons (such as <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interracial_marriage" title="Interracial marriage">interracial</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interreligious_marriage" title="Interreligious marriage">interreligious marriages</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murrow_2010_397.E2.80.93412_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murrow_2010_397.E2.80.93412-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cohabitation, sometimes called de facto marriage, is becoming more commonly known as a substitute for conventional marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Common-law_marriage_in_the_United_States" title="Common-law marriage in the United States">Common-law marriage in the United States</a> can still be contracted in nine US states, and in two others under restriction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This helps provide the surviving partner a legal basis for inheriting the deceased's belongings in the event of the death of their cohabiting partner. In today's cohabiting relationships, forty percent of households include children, giving us an idea of how cohabitation could be considered a new normative type of family dynamic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohabitation_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohabitation-28">[28]</a></sup> As of 2012, 41% of all births in the US were to unmarried women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In three states (Mississippi - 55%, Louisiana - 53%, and New Mexico - 52%) births outside marriage were in the majority; the lowest percentage of births outside marriage was in Utah, at 19%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> During the period 2006 - 2010, 58% of births outside of marriage were to cohabiting parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_objections_to_cohabitation">Contemporary objections to cohabitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Contemporary objections to cohabitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Contemporary objections to cohabiting couples include religious opposition to non-marital unions, social pressure for couples to get married, and potential effects of cohabitation on a child's development.</p>
<p>The rise in the number of cohabiting couples and children born out of wedlock in the Western world has made cohabitation a strong focus of sociological research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-germanypmc_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-germanypmc-39">[39]</a></sup> The rise in cohabiting couples in the United States, from around 450,000 in 1960 to 7.5 million in 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> has been accompanied by US research performed on child development within cohabiting households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunifon_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunifon-41">[41]</a></sup> Opponents of cohabitation say non-marital parenting is an unsuitable environment for a child's development. One study from 2002 correlated lower numeracy skills and higher delinquency to children of cohabiting couples,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunifon_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunifon-41">[41]</a></sup> however, recent studies that control for factors including poverty, the educational level of parents and violence in the home show children of cohabiting couples are developmentally similar to peers of comparable married couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_children">Effect on children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Effect on children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2001, researchers compared teenage children living in a cohabiting household against peers in single-parent households. The results showed Caucasian and Hispanic teenagers had lower performance in school, greater risk of suspension or expulsion than peers from single-parent households, and the same rate behavioral and emotional problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study on the 1995 and 2002 National Survey of Family Growth found increases in both the prevalence and duration of unmarried cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19119426_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19119426-44">[44]</a></sup> The study found that 40% of children in the United States would live in a cohabiting household by age 12, and children born to single mothers were more likely than those born to married mothers to live in a cohabiting household. The percentage of women ages 19−44 who had ever cohabited increased from 45% in 1995 to 54% in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19119426_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19119426-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, 63% of women who graduate from high school were found to spend some time cohabiting, compared to only 45% of women with a 4-year college degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19119426_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19119426-44">[44]</a></sup> Cohabiting couples who have children often get married. One study found that children born of parents who cohabit are 90% more likely to end up living in households with married parents than children born to single mothers. 67% of unmarried Hispanic mothers are expected to marry, while 40% of African American mothers are expected to marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19119426_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19119426-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">Fornication</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Marriage#Marriage_and_religion" title="Marriage">Marriage § Marriage and religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extramarital_sex#Religions" title="Extramarital sex">Extramarital sex § Religions</a></div>
<p>Studies have found that religious affiliation correlates with cohabitation and marriage entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peer2011_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-peer2011-45">[45]</a></sup> People frequently cite religious reasons for their opposition to cohabitation. The Roman Catholic Church and nearly all mainstream Protestant denominations around the world oppose cohabitation and consider it to be the sin of <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">fornication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prager_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prager-48">[48]</a></sup> However, others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican Church</a> "welcome cohabiting couples in the Church and encourage them to regard cohabitation as a prelude to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_marriage" title="Christian marriage">Christian marriage</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Religion can also lead to societal pressures against cohabitation especially within highly religious communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Some couples may refrain from cohabitation because one or both partners fear disappointing or alienating conservative family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peer2011_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-peer2011-45">[45]</a></sup> Young adults who grew up in families that oppose cohabitation have lower rates than their peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The increase in cohabitation in the United States and other developed nations has been linked to the <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a> of those countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Researchers have noted that changes in the religious demographics of a society have accompanied the rise in cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-marital and same-sex relationships are forbidden by the Islamic law of <a href="/wiki/Zina" title="Zina">Zina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and cohabitation is against the law in many Muslim majority countries including Saudi Arabia, Pakistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Afghanistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-travel.state.gov_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-travel.state.gov-57">[57]</a></sup> Iran,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-travel.state.gov_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-travel.state.gov-57">[57]</a></sup> Kuwait,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Maldives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Morocco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Oman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Mauritania,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> United Arab Emirates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Sudan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> and Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_marriage_and_family_life">Effects on marriage and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Effects on marriage and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Likelihood_of_split">Likelihood of split</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Likelihood of split">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As premarital cohabitation occurs more frequently, there are more research participants which has led to a greater understanding of the factors of stable, lasting relationships. This has led to more possibilities and studies. The early studies, performed decades ago, did not account for the multiple factors that comprise the stability of a relationship. Researchers have found evidence that previous research into the effects of premarital cohabitation does not apply to modern relationships. New results have shown there are many factors that lead to divorce that are not cohabitation.</p>
<p>Conflicting studies on the effect of cohabitation on subsequent marriage have been published. In countries where the majority of people disapprove of unmarried individuals living together, or a minority of the population cohabits before marriage, marriages resulting from cohabitation are more prone to divorce. But in a study on European countries, those where around half of the population cohabits before marriage, cohabitation is not selective of divorce-prone individuals, and no difference in couples that have cohabited before and after marriage is observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16889125_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16889125-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In countries such as Italy, the increased risk of marital disruption for people who experienced premarital cohabitation can be entirely attributed to the selection of the most divorce-prone into cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22707812_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22707812-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control" title="Centers for Disease Control">CDC</a> found that for married couples the likelihood percentage of the relationship ending after 5 years is 20%, for unmarried cohabitators the likelihood percentage is 49%. After 10 years the likelihood percentage for the relationship to end is 33% for married couples and 62% for unmarried cohabitators. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> One German study found that in regions with high rates of childbirth to cohabitating parents, no negative effect is observed in cohabitation. The study states "union stability of cohabiting mothers is positively related to their prevalence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-germanypmc_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-germanypmc-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2004 study of 136 couples (272 individuals) from researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Denver" title="University of Denver">University of Denver</a> found differences among couples that cohabited before engagement, after engagement, or not until marriage. The <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_study" title="Longitudinal study">longitudinal study</a> collected survey data collected before marriage and 10 months into marriage, with findings suggesting those who cohabit before engagement are at greater risk for poor marital outcomes than those who cohabit only after engagement or at marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> A follow-up <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_survey" title="Statistical survey">survey</a> by the researches of over 1,000 married men and women married in the past 10 years found those who moved in with a lover before engagement or marriage reported significantly lower quality marriages and a greater possibility of a separation than other couples. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19203165_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19203165-73">[73]</a></sup> About 20 percent of those who cohabited before getting <a href="/wiki/Engagement" title="Engagement">engaged</a> had since suggested splitting - compared with only 12 percent of those who only moved in together after getting engaged and 10 percent who did not cohabit prior to marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph.co.uk_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph.co.uk-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The researchers from Denver suggest that relationships with pre-engagement cohabitation "may wind up sliding into marriage",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph.co.uk_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph.co.uk-74">[74]</a></sup> whereas those that only cohabit post engagement or marriage make a more clear decision. This could explain their 2006 study of 197 heterosexual couples finding that men who cohabited with their spouse before engagement were less dedicated than men who cohabited only after engagement or not at all before marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17176189_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17176189-75">[75]</a></sup> In some heterosexual couples, women are more likely to understand cohabitation as an intermediary step preceding marriage, and men more likely to perceive it without an explicit connection to marriage. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21643456_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21643456-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>An analysis of data from the CDC's <a href="/wiki/National_Survey_of_Family_Growth" title="National Survey of Family Growth">National Survey of Family Growth</a> data from 1988, 1995, and 2002 suggests that the positive relationship between premarital cohabitation and marital instability has weakened for more recent birth and marriage cohorts, as the total number of couples cohabiting before marriage has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20879685_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20879685-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later CDC work found that between 2002 and 2006-2010, the number of couples in opposite-sex cohabiting relationships increased from 9.0% to 11.2% for women, and from 9.2% to 12.2% for men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Drawing on the 2006-2008 data, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princeton_university" title="Princeton university">Princeton university</a> researchers examined whether and to what extent variation in premarital cohabitation experiences influence marital stability. They found that the relationship between cohabitation and marital instability is complex and depends in part on marriage cohort, race/ethnicity, and marriage plans. Their analyses reveal that a 'cohabitation effect' exists only for women married prior to 1996, and that, until marriage plans are considered, there is no cohabitation effect among women married since 1996. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent research from 2011 by the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> has found that the number of couples that cohabit before marriage has increased. 44% of adults (and more than half of 30- to 49-year-olds) say they have cohabited at some point. Nearly two-thirds of adults who ever cohabited (64%) say they thought about it as a step toward marriage. The report also notes a trend toward rising public acceptance of cohabiting couples over the years. Most Americans now say the rise in unmarried couples living together either makes no difference to society (46%) or is good for society (9%). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2012 study found that, among cohabiting individuals, those who were engaged prior to cohabitation or had "definite plans for marriage" were linked to lower risks of marital instability among women, but the relationship was not observed with men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>One study on low to moderate income couples living with minor children found that respondents who became sexually involved within the first month of their relationship were correlated to lower scores of relationship quality among women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Another study found respondents to a mail-in survey self-reported higher levels of commitment in the cohabiting group, as well as lower relationship satisfaction and more negative communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abuse_and_infidelity">Abuse and infidelity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Abuse and infidelity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>University of Chicago sociologist Linda Waite <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chronicle.uchicago.edu_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle.uchicago.edu-86">[86]</a></sup> found that "16 percent of cohabiting women reported that arguments with their partners became physical during the past year, while only 5 percent of married women had similar experiences." Most cohabiting couples have a faithful relationship, but Waite's surveys also demonstrated that 20 percent of cohabiting women reported having secondary sex partners, compared to only 4 percent of married women. A 1992 study found that male members of heterosexual couples with children are less likely to be a part of the childcare but half the time they are responsible for child abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1393717_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1393717-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to an article by Judith Treas and Deirdre Giesen, cohabiting couples are twice as likely to experience infidelity within the relationship than married couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility">Fertility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Fertility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regarding cohabitation as a <a href="/wiki/Fertility_factor_(demography)" title="Fertility factor (demography)">fertility factor</a>, a large survey in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> came to the result that <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">married</a> women had an average of 1.9 children, compared to 1.3 among those cohabiting. The corresponding numbers for men were 1.7 and 1.1, respectively. The difference of 0.6 children for both sexes was expected to decrease to between 0.2 and 0.3 over the lifetime when correcting for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confounder" title="Confounder">confounder</a> that married people have their children earlier in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us2012_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-us2012-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study of the United States and multiple countries in Europe came to the result that women who continue to cohabit after birth have significantly lower probability of having a second child than married women in all countries except those in Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Another study, on the contrary, came to the result that cohabiting couples in France have equal fertility as married ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balbo2013_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-balbo2013-91">[91]</a></sup> Also, Russians have a higher fertility within cohabitation, while Romanians rather tend to have childless marriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Survey data from 2003 in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> came to the result that marriage equalized the total fertility rate among both highly educated and low educated people to approximately 1.4. Among those cohabiting, on the other hand, lower level of education increased fertility rate to 1.7, and a higher level of education decreased it to 0.7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> On the other hand, another study came to the result that Romanian women with little education have about equal fertility in marital and cohabiting partnerships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_effects">Financial effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Financial effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, married couples that submit a combined tax return may face a <a href="/wiki/Marriage_penalty" title="Marriage penalty">marriage penalty</a>, where tax credits for low-income single earners are not applied to the combined income. In October 1998, Senate GOP leader <a href="/wiki/Trent_Lott" title="Trent Lott">Trent Lott</a> decided to pull a bill to abolish "the <a href="/wiki/Marriage_penalty" title="Marriage penalty">marriage penalty</a>," "which in the tax code reflects the fact that married couples who both work for wages frequently pay more in taxes than if they earned the same amount of income but weren't married. And the more equal the incomes of the couple, the steeper the marriage tax penalty." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-americanvalues.org_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-americanvalues.org-95">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Earned_income_tax_credit" title="Earned income tax credit">earned income tax credit</a> (EITC) is cash welfare for low-income workers, but the problem is the EITC is not for married couples because they have to combine their wages, which again leads to "the marriage penalty." If couples do not get married then their wages do not have to combine and the EITC in a way is "paying for" low-income couples not to marry. Opponents of cohabitation believe that some cohabiting couples choose not to marry because they would suffer a tax penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-americanvalues.org_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-americanvalues.org-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the perceived disincentive to marry that the EITC provides, cohabiting couples suffer many financial losses as their unions are not recognized with the same legal and financial benefits as those who are legally married. These financial penalties can include the costs of separate insurance policies and the costs of setting up legal protections similar to those that are automatically granted by the state upon marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No_effect">No effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: No effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A conflicting study, published by the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Health_Statistics" title="National Center for Health Statistics">National Center for Health Statistics</a>, with a sample of 12,571 people, concludes that "those who live together after making plans to marry or getting engaged have about the same chances of divorcing as couples who never cohabited before marriage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, William Doherty, a professor in the Department of Family Social Science at the University of Minnesota has remarked that in his research he has found that "committed cohabiting relationships seem to confer many of the benefits of marriage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2003 study by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Institute_of_Family_Studies" title="Australian Institute of Family Studies">Australian Institute of Family Studies</a> found that "The differences in measured outcomes for those from direct and indirect marriages appear to be entirely attributable to other factors." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The study concluded that the evidence suggests that premarital cohabitation has "little impact one way or the other" on the chances of any subsequent marriage surviving.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_region">By region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: By region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quebec-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="280" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Quebec-map.png/220px-Quebec-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ab/Quebec-map.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quebec-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cohabitation is very common in Quebec. Since 1995, the majority of births in this province are outside of marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat.gouv.qc.ca_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat.gouv.qc.ca-100">[100]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> laws on the recognition of unmarried cohabitation for legal purposes vary significantly by province/territory; and in addition to this, federal regulations also have an impact across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Common-law_marriage#Canada" title="Common-law marriage">Common-law marriage#Canada</a>) Family formation has undergone significant changes in Canada during the last decades of the 20th century, but the patterns vary widely across the country, suggesting differing cultural norms in different regions. Since the mid 1990s, births to cohabiting parents have increased, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In Canada, it is difficult to obtain exact data on the percentage of births outside of marriage, due to the fact that data on the marital status of mothers is collected differently across the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">provinces and territories of Canada</a>, and in some (such as <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>) it is not broken down in detail in regard to whether the mother was legally married or not at the birth of her child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> As of 2012, the statistical category of "single mothers"(defined as <i>never married</i> at the time of the birth) encompassed 28.3% of mothers, the category "divorced" (i.e. mothers who were unmarried at the time of birth, but had been previously married during their lives) encompassed 1%, while for 10% of mothers the marital status was unknown ("not stated").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www5.statcan.gc.ca_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-www5.statcan.gc.ca-105">[105]</a></sup> There are, however, very significant differences by province/territory; for example in 2012, 77.8% of births in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a> were listed to "single mothers", by contrast, less that 20% of mothers in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> were listed in this category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www5.statcan.gc.ca_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-www5.statcan.gc.ca-105">[105]</a></sup> Latest data from the <a href="/wiki/Institut_de_la_statistique_du_Qu%C3%A9bec" title="Institut de la statistique du Québec">Quebec Statistical Institute</a> shows that as of 2015, in Quebec, 63% of children were born to unmarried women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In Canada, legal issues regarding cohabitation are very complicated by the fact that <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a> in this regard differs by province/territory, which is very confusing to the public,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> especially as this contrasts to <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law" title="Criminal law">criminal law</a> which is the same across Canada, as well as to marriage and divorce law, which is also the same across the country, under the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Divorce_Act_(Canada)" title="Divorce Act (Canada)">Divorce Act (Canada)</a> (although provinces/territories have jurisdiction over some marital issues, including the solemnization of marriage, spousal and child support, and property division).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Marital_status" title="Marital status">marital status</a> of Canadians also varies by province/territory: in 2011, 46.4% of the population aged 15 and over was legally married; ranging from the lowest percentage of married people being in Nunavut (29.7%), Northwest Territories (35.0%), Quebec (35.4%), and Yukon (37.6%); to highest being in Newfoundland and Labrador (52.9%), Prince Edward Island (51.7%), Ontario (50.3%) and Alberta (50.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> While today Quebec is known for liberal family formation and cohabitation, this is a recent development: during the first half of the 20th century, family life in the province was conservative and strongly dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a>; before 1968, there was no provincial divorce legislation in Quebec, and spouses could only end their marriage if they obtained a private <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> One of the explanations of the current high rates of cohabitation in Quebec is that the traditionally strong social control of the church and the Catholic doctrine over people's private relations and sexual morality has led the population to rebel against traditional and conservative social values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> While some provinces were early to modernize family law, in others this only happened in the 1990s and the 21st century, such as in <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Family_Law_Act_(Alberta)" title="Family Law Act (Alberta)">Family Law Act (Alberta)</a> which came into force in 2005. This Act overhauled family legislation, replacing the Domestic Relations Act, the Maintenance Order Act, the Parentage and Maintenance Act, and parts of the Provincial Court Act and the Child, Youth and Family Enhancement Act, which were seen as outdated. Also, the <a href="/wiki/Adult_interdependent_relationship_in_Alberta" title="Adult interdependent relationship in Alberta">Adult Interdependent Relationships Act (S.A. 2002, c. A-4.5)</a> amended 69 Alberta laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Prairies" title="Canadian Prairies">Canadian Prairies</a> provinces of <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> have strong common-law spouses regulations, imposing rights and obligations on common-law couples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> has also been very slow to advance family law - it was only in 1999 that this province abolished discrimination against "illegitimate" children with regard to inheritance (through section 16 of NS Intestate Succession Act amended in 1999).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In general, today, provinces in <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">Western Canada</a> give more rights to common-law spouses than those in <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Canada" title="Atlantic Canada">Atlantic Canada</a> and in Quebec. This may seem quite paradoxical, because it is the eastern provinces which have the strongest tradition of cohabitation; according to a study "unmarried cohabitation seems to be more common in Eastern Canada than in Western Canada, which might be related to internal and international migration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> (as of 2012, 48% of births in <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, 47.1% in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, and 45.2% in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, were listed to "single mothers", way above the national average).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www5.statcan.gc.ca_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-www5.statcan.gc.ca-105">[105]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, the Family Law Act came into force in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cohabitation_Approval_General_Social_Survey_1994-2012_v2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="286" data-file-width="320" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0f/Cohabitation_Approval_General_Social_Survey_1994-2012_v2.png/220px-Cohabitation_Approval_General_Social_Survey_1994-2012_v2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/0f/Cohabitation_Approval_General_Social_Survey_1994-2012_v2.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cohabitation_Approval_General_Social_Survey_1994-2012_v2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Public approval in the United States for cohabiting couples has risen since 1994<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roper2014_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-roper2014-118">[118]</a></sup>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #004586; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Agree</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffd320; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Neither agree nor disagree</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff420e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Disagree</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cohabitation_in_the_United_States" title="Cohabitation in the United States">Cohabitation in the United States</a> became common in the late 20th century. As of 2005<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 4.85 million unmarried couples were living together, and as of 2002<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, about half of all women aged 15 to 44 had lived unmarried with a partner. In 2007, it is estimated that 6.4 million households were maintained by two opposite sex persons who said they were unmarried. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> In 2012, the General Social Survey found that public disapproval of cohabitation had dropped to 20% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roper2014_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-roper2014-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers at the National Center for Family and Marriage Research estimated in 2011 that 66% of first marriages are entered after a period of cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> According to the 2009 American Community Survey conducted by the Census Bureau, the proportion of 30- to 44-year-olds living together has almost doubled since 1999, from 4% to 7%. Fifty-eight percent of women aged 19 to 44 had ever cohabited in data collected in 2006-08, while in 1987 only 33% had. Cohabitation is more prevalent among those with less education. "Among women ages 19 to 44, 73% of those without a high school education have ever cohabited, compared with about half of women with some college (52%) or a college degree (47%)," note the Pew study's authors, Richard Fry and D'Vera Cohn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the mid-20th century, laws against cohabitation, fornication, adultery and other such behaviors were common in the US (especially in Southern and Northeastern states), but these laws have been gradually abolished or struck down by courts as unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>"Cohabitation was almost impossible in the United States prior to the 1960s. Laws prevented unmarried couples from registering in hotels and it was very difficult for an unmarried couple to obtain a home mortgage. From 1960 to 1998, cohabitation moved from disreputable and difficult to normal and convenient." <a class="external text" href="http://www.pbs.org/fmc/timeline/ddisruption.htm" rel="nofollow">PBS: Social disruptions</a></li>
</ul>
<p>As of April 2016, cohabitation of unmarried couples remains illegal in three states (<a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">fornication</a> remains illegal in six states (Idaho,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Utah,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> South Carolina,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Minnesota,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Massachusetts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Illinois<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>). These laws are almost never enforced and are now believed to be unconstitutional since the legal decision <i><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_v._Texas" title="Lawrence v. Texas">Lawrence v. Texas</a></i> in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> However, these laws may have indirect effects. For example, one consequence may be that one may not claim their boyfriend/girlfriend as a dependent (for a tax exemption), whereas in the other states it may be possible to do so after meeting 4 criteria: residency, income, support and status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, in North Carolina, Pender County Superior Court judge Benjamin G. Alford ruled that North Carolina's cohabitation law is unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_North_Carolina" title="Supreme Court of North Carolina">Supreme Court of North Carolina</a> has never had the opportunity to rule on it, so the law's statewide constitutionality remains unclear.</p>
<p>On 13 December 2013, <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_judge" title="United States federal judge">US Federal Judge</a> <a href="/wiki/Clark_Waddoups" title="Clark Waddoups">Clark Waddoups</a> ruled in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Buhman" title="Brown v. Buhman">Brown v. Buhman</a></i> that the portions of Utah's anti-<a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamy</a> laws which prohibit multiple cohabitation were unconstitutional, but also allowed Utah to maintain its ban on multiple marriage licenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Unlawful cohabitation, where prosecutors did not need to prove that a marriage ceremony had taken place (only that a couple had lived together), had been a major tool used to prosecute polygamy in Utah since the 1882 <a href="/wiki/Edmunds_Act" title="Edmunds Act">Edmunds Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cohabitation in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> is becoming more common. Indeed, although this is a largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> region, it has the highest rates of non-marital childbearing in the world (55–74% of all children in this region are born to unmarried parents).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> In Mexico, 18.7% of all couples were cohabiting as of 2005<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. Among young people, the figures are much higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanier_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanier-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2000, in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> 58% of births were to unmarried women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustaindemographicdividend.org_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-sustaindemographicdividend.org-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The percentage of births outside marriage has increased throughout Latin America during the past decades, and there is also a relation to place of residence: women living in the capital city are more likely to have children outside of marriage than those living in other parts of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Recent data shows figures for non-marital childbearing to be 74% for <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, 69% for <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, 68% for <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, 66% for <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and 55% for <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sustaindemographicdividend.org_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-sustaindemographicdividend.org-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, living together is socially acceptable only after marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> However, cohabitation is an emerging trend in urban areas of Nepal. Reports have shown that there may be significant number of unmarried couples cohabiting in cities, especially in the capital, <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>. Even when the unmarried couples cohabit they either prefer to remain anonymous or pose themselves as married couple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Cohabitation is not recognized by the law of Nepal and there is no special provision to secure the right of cohabitants in Nepalese law.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> cohabitation after divorce is frequently punished by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Salishi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Salishi (page does not exist)">salishi</a> system of informal courts, especially in rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Cohabitation_in_India" title="Cohabitation in India">Cohabitation in India</a></b> had been taboo since British rule. However, this is no longer true in large cities, but is not often found in rural areas which are more conservative. <b>Live-in</b> relationships are legal in India. Recent Indian court rulings have ascribed some rights to long-term cohabiting partners. Female live-in partners have economic rights under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protection_of_Women_from_Domestic_Violence_Act_2005" title="Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act 2005">Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act 2005</a> subject to following conditions as laid by Honourable <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_India" title="Supreme Court of India">Supreme Court of India</a> in case of <i>D. Velusamy v D. Patchaiammal</i>:</li>
</ul>
<p>(a) The couple must hold themselves out to society as being akin to spouses. (b) They must be of legal age to marry. (c) They must be otherwise qualified to enter into a legal marriage, including being unmarried. (d) They must have voluntarily cohabited and held themselves out to the world as being akin to spouses for a significant period of time.</p>
<ul>
<li>In Indonesia, an Islamic penal code proposed in 2005 would have made cohabitation punishable by up to two years in prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> The practice is still frowned upon, and many hotels and boarding houses have been raided by police for allowed unmarried couples to share a room.</li>
<li>In Japan, according to M. Iwasawa at the National Institute of Population and Social Security Research, less than 3% of females between 25-29 are currently cohabiting, but more than 1 in 5 have had some experience of an unmarried partnership, including cohabitation. A more recent Iwasawa study has shown that there has been a recent emergence of non-marital cohabitation. Couples born in the 1950s cohort showed an incidence of cohabitation of 11.8%, where the 1960s and 1970s cohorts showed cohabitation rates of 30%, and 53.9% respectively. The split between urban and rural residence for people who had cohabited is indicates 68.8% were urban and 31.2% were rural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, around 2.4 million Filipinos were cohabiting as of 2004<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. The 2000 census placed the percentage of cohabiting couples at 19%. The majority of individuals are between the ages of 20-24. Poverty was often the main factor in decision to cohabit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, cohabitation is very common. In 2012, 40% of all births in the EU 28 countries were extramarital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa.eu_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-europa.eu-151">[151]</a></sup> The percentage of births outside marriage has increased dramatically in many European countries during the last few decades, as can be seen in the figure. The majority of births occur outside of marriage (figures as of 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-152">[152]</a></sup> except where otherwise stipulated) in the following European countries: <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> (66.9%), <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> (59% in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> (58.8% in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsi.bg_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-nsi.bg-154">[154]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> (58.3% - in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (57.1% in 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (55.2% in 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europa.eu_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-europa.eu-151">[151]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (54.6% in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-157">[157]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (52.3%), <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (52.5% in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-157">[157]</a></sup>), and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (50.7% in 2015 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>).</li>
<li>While couples of all ages cohabit, the phenomenon is much more common among younger people. In late 2005, 21% of families in Finland consisted of cohabiting couples (all age groups). Of couples with children, 18% were cohabiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Of ages 18 and above in 2003, 13.4% were cohabiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Generally, cohabitation amongst Finns is most common for people under 30. Legal obstacles for cohabitation were removed in 1926 in a reform of the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Code_of_Finland" title="Criminal Code of Finland">Criminal Code</a>, while the phenomenon was socially accepted much later on. In France, 17.5% of couples were cohabiting as of 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanier_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vanier-140">[140]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Britain today, nearly half of babies are born to people who are not married (in the United Kingdom 47.3% in 2011;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epp.eurostat.ec.europa.eu_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-epp.eurostat.ec.europa.eu-161">[161]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in 2012 the proportion was 51.3%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>). It is estimated that by 2016, the majority of births in the UK will be to unmarried parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> of the late 19th century is famous for the Victorian standards of personal morality. Historians generally agree that the middle classes held high personal moral standards and rejected cohabitation. They have debated whether the working classes followed suit. Moralists in the late 19th century such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mayhew" title="Henry Mayhew">Henry Mayhew</a> decried high levels of cohabitation without marriage and illegitimate births in London slums. However new research using computerized matching of data files shows that the rates of cohabitation were quite low—under 5% -- for the working class and the urban poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to the increase in cohabitation, several legal changes were made in the UK in recent years. In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the Family Law (Scotland) Act 2006 provides cohabitants with some limited rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In addition, since 2003 in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, 2002 in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, and 2006 in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, an unmarried father has <a href="/wiki/Parental_responsibility_(access_and_custody)" title="Parental responsibility (access and custody)">parental responsibility</a> if he is listed on the <a href="/wiki/Birth_certificate" title="Birth certificate">birth certificate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are differences in extramarital births by region; in 2012, the highest percentage of births to unmarried women were in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_East_of_England" title="North East of England">North East of England</a> at 59%, and in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> at 58%; and the lowest in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> (36%) and in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> (42%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> One of the reasons cited for the lower percentage of extramarital births in London is the high number of immigrants from conservative world regions who reside in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Younger mothers are more likely to be unmarried: in 2011, 96% of mothers aged under 20 were unmarried, compared to only 31% of mothers in their 30s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> A study dealing with births that occurred in the year 2000 found several social and personal characteristics of cohabiting vs. married parents: married mothers are most likely to be Asian and least likely Black Caribbean; married mothers are more religious; married mothers and fathers are more educated (they are more than twice as likely to have a degree than cohabiting parents); married fathers are twice as likely to have a professional occupation as cohabiting fathers; married parents have a better financial situation; married mothers are older and are more likely to have had the birth planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> A 2006 study found that cohabiting couples, with and without children, are the fastest-growing family type in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Cohabiting couples who live with their children are more common in the North of England than in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the UK, in recent years, the falling marriage rates and increased births outside of marriage have become a political issue, with questions of whether the government should promote marriage (i.e. though tax benefits or public campaigns) or whether it should focus on the status of a parent, rather than that of a spouse; with the former view being endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>, and the latter by the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats" title="Liberal Democrats">Liberal Democrats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> There are also differences between <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, with the latter being more accepting of cohabitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Bulgaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Bulgaria, there has been a rapid increase in cohabitation after the fall of Communism. The transition from communism to market economy had a great impact on the demographic behavior of the population. After the fall of Communism, the legal and social pressure to get married has declined, and the population has started to experience new life styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-researchgate.net_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-researchgate.net-16">[16]</a></sup> As of 2014, 58.8% of children were born to unmarried mothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nsi.bg_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-nsi.bg-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_Republic">Czech Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Czech Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The marriage rates in Czech Republic have fallen dramatically during the past decades. In the 1970s to 1980s, about 96%-97% of women got married; in 2000 it was estimated that only 75% of women would ever get married.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paa2004.princeton.edu_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-paa2004.princeton.edu-176">[176]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_at_first_marriage" title="Age at first marriage">age at first marriage</a> for women has increased from being in the range of 21.4 - 21.8 years in the 1970s and 1980s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paa2004.princeton.edu_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-paa2004.princeton.edu-176">[176]</a></sup> to being 29.6 in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> In the early 1990s, predictions were made by some Czech demographers that cohabitation would increase during the next decades; and indeed, there has been a marked increase in the number of people who live in non-marital couple relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> In 2015, 47.8% of births were to unmarried women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cohabitation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As in other western societies, patterns of family life have been changing in Germany during the past decades.
